Mutual Claims of Partial Emancipation: Earning Obligation and Hadith Proof
Ruling on partial emancipation with mutual claims and hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mutual claims of partial emancipation, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of the emancipator's wealth, and apply the hadith evidence to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
In partial emancipation disputes, the slave is freed and must earn for both partners; wealth of the claimant does not negate earning.

This lesson examines a scenario where two partners each claim to have emancipated the other's share of a jointly owned slave. The ruling turns on whether the emancipator's wealth affects the slave's obligation to earn (kitabah). We will explore the Hanafi position, the Sahibayn's objection, and the key hadith evidence used to support the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and core dispute
Two partners each claim to have emancipated the other's share. What does Abu Hanifa rule regarding the slave's status and obligations?
2
Understand the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differentiate based on the wealth of the claimant. How does their position differ from Abu Hanifa's?
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the underlying principles
The ruling is built on two principles: divisibility of emancipation and whether the emancipator's wealth prevents earning. Grasp how these principles lead to the different views.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's analogy
Abu Hanifa uses the analogy of a garment blown into another's dye: the owner pays regardless of wealth. How does this analogy support his position on earning?
The slave is analogized to the garment owner; the slave's poverty is considered, so he earns rather than pays cash.
5
Evaluate the hadith evidence and reconciliation
Both sides cite hadith. The Hanafis reconcile the opposing narrations by interpreting 'thumma' (then) and 'waw' (and) as not negating the possibility of earning after assessment. See how this reconciles all texts.
Key: 'thumma' indicates delay, not immediate freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العبد الذي يعتق بعضه ولو شهد كل بعتق نصيب صاحبه سعى لهما والولاء لهما وهذا كله عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا ليس له إلا الضمان مع اليسار، أو السعاية مع الإعسار، ولا يرجع المعتق على العبد بشيء، والولاء للمعتق في الوجهين وعند الثلاثة في الموسر كذلك، وفي المعسر يبقى شريكه كما كان، فللشريك بيع نصيبه وهبته وعتقه وهذا مبني على أصلين: أحدهما: تجزؤ الإعتاق وعدمه وقد مر بيانه والثاني: أن يسار المعتق لا يمنع السعاية عنده، وعندهما يمنع؛ لقوله عليه في الرجل يعتق لقيمته: "إن كان غنياً ضمن وإن كان فقيراً سعى في حصة الآخر" قسم، والقسمة تنافي الشركة، وله: أنه احتبست مالية نصيبه عند العبد، فله أن يضمنه كما إذا هبت الريح بثوب إنسان وألقته في صبغ غيره، حتى انصبغ به، فعلى صاحب الثوب قيمة صبغه موسراً كان أو معسراً، فكذا هنا، غير أن العبد فقير فيستسعيه. ثم المعتبر التيسير لا يسار العتاد، وهو أن يملك من المال قيمة نصيب الآخر فاضلاً عما يحتاج إليه من ملبوسه ونفقة عياله وسكناه، ويعتبر حاله يوم الإعتاق حتى لو أيسر لعبده أو أعسر لا يعتبر، وإن اختلفا فيه يحكم الحال، إلا أن يكون بين الخصومة والعتق مدة تختلف فيها الأحوال، فيكون القول قول المعتق؛ لأنه منكر، وإن اختلفا في قيمة العبد يوم العتق، فإن كان قائماً يقوم للحال، وإن كان هالكاً فالقول للمعتق؛ لأنه منكر، واحتجت الثلاثة بقوله عليه: "من أعتق عبداً بين اثنين فإن كان موسراً قوم عليه ثم يعتق"، رواه البخاري، ولقوله عليه: "من أعتق شركا له في عبد وكان له ما يبلغ ثمن العبد قوم العبد عليه قيمة عدل فأعطى شركائه حصصهم، وعتق عليه، وإلا فقد عتق منه ما عتق"، رواه البخاري ومسلم. ولنا قوله عليه: "من أعتق شقصاً له في مملوك فخلاصه عليه في ماله إن كان له مال وإلا قوم عليه واستسعي به غير مشقوق" أي لا يشدد عليه الأمر، رواه البخاري ومسلم فثبت السعاية بذلك، وقال ابن حزم على ثبوت الاستسعاء ثلاثون صحابياً، وليس فيما رووه ما ينافي مذهبنا بل فيه دليل على ما نقول؛ لأنه عليه قال في الحديث الأول: "فإن كان موسراً قوم عليه ثم يعتق" وكلمة "ثم" للتراخي، فدل على أنه يعتق بعد ذلك، إما بعتقه أو بالسعاية، وقال في الحديث الثاني: "أعطى شركاءه حصصهم وعتق عليه بالواو وهي لا تنافي الترتيب ولا التراخي، فحملناه عليه توفيقاً بين الأحاديث
Chapter on the slave who is partially emancipated. And if each witnesses to the emancipation of the other's share, the slave performs kitabah for them both and the wala' is for them both. This is all according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: He only has the option of compensation if wealthy, or earning if poor. The emancipator does not claim anything from the slave. Wala' is for the emancipator in both cases. According to the three, in the case of wealth it is similar, and in the case of poverty the partner remains as he was, so the partner may sell, gift, or emancipate his share. This is based on two principles: one, the divisibility of emancipation and its opposite; the second, that the wealth of the emancipator does not prevent earning according to him, but according to them it does. Due to his statement regarding the man who emancipates for his price: 'If he was wealthy, he guarantees; if poor, the slave earns for the other's share.' He divided, and division negates partnership. And his argument: the value of his share has remained with the slave, so he may guarantee it, just as when the wind blows a person's garment and throws it into another's dye, the owner of the garment must pay the value of the dye, whether wealthy or poor. Similarly here, except that the slave is poor, so he makes him earn. Then consideration is of ease, not wealth of readiness; he must own the value of the other's share in excess of his needs for clothing, family maintenance, and housing. His state on the day of emancipation is considered; even if he later becomes wealthy or poor, it is not considered. If they dispute, the current state is judged unless there is a period between the dispute and emancipation in which states differ, then the word of the emancipator is taken because he is denying. If they dispute over the value of the slave on the day of emancipation, if he is present he is valued currently; if destroyed, the word of the emancipator is taken because he is denying. And the three argued with his statement: 'Whoever emancipates a slave between two partners, if he is wealthy, he is assessed then emancipated,' narrated by Bukhari; and his statement: 'Whoever emancipates a share in a slave and has enough to cover the price, the slave is assessed at a fair price, he gives the partners their shares, and the slave is emancipated; otherwise, only what has been emancipated is emancipated,' narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. And our evidence is his statement: 'Whoever emancipates a portion in a slave, his release is upon him from his wealth if he has wealth; otherwise, he is assessed and made to earn without hardship,' narrated by Bukhari and Muslim, so earning is established. Ibn Hazm said thirty companions affirmed the establishment of earning. There is nothing in their narrations contrary to our madhhab; rather it is evidence for our view. He said in the first hadith: 'If wealthy, assessed then emancipated' - the word 'then' indicates delay, so it shows he is emancipated after that, either by his emancipation or by earning. And in the second hadith: 'He gives partners their shares and the slave is emancipated' with the conjunction 'and', which does not negate order or delay, so we interpret it accordingly to reconcile the hadith.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on partial emancipation. The scenario: two partners each claim to have emancipated the other's share of a jointly owned slave. According to Abu Hanifa, the slave is immediately free and must perform kitabah (earning) for both partners, and the wala' belongs to both. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the slave is free only if the claiming partner is wealthy, in which case he must compensate the other partner with a guarantee (daman); if poor, the slave must earn for the other's share. The passage explains the underlying principles: (1) whether emancipation can be divided, and (2) whether the wealth of the emancipator prevents the requirement of earning. Abu Hanifa argues by analogy: just as the owner of a garment blown into another's dye must pay for the dye regardless of wealth, here the partner's share value is retained by the slave, so the partner may guarantee it. However, since the slave is poor, earning is required. The criteria for wealth is having surplus beyond basic needs on the day of emancipation. The hadith proof is detailed: the opposing school (the three) use hadith that seem to require only guarantee or assessment. The Hanafi response interprets the word 'then' (thumma) to indicate delay, so emancipation can occur later via earning. The conjunction 'and' (waw) does not contradict this. Thus the Hanafi view is supported by reconciling the narrations.

Key Terms
سعى (sa'ā): The slave performs kitabah (earning) by working to pay for the remaining share.
ولاء (walā'): The legal bond of patronage between the emancipator and freed slave, entailing inheritance rights and responsibility.
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee or compensation paid by the wealthy claiming partner to the other partner for the value of his share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if two partners each claim to have emancipated the other's share of a jointly owned slave, what is the status of the slave?
A) The slave remains in joint ownership because the claims cancel out.
B) The slave is freed immediately and must earn for both partners.
C) The slave becomes a mudabbar (future emancipation upon death).
D) The slave becomes the property of the qadi (judge).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, partial emancipation claims result in immediate freedom with an obligation to earn, regardless of the wealth of the claimant.

Oath on Buying a Bunch of Dates with Mixed Types
Ruling on oath: purchase vs. eating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath regarding buying dates of mixed types and explain the distinction between purchase and eating based on the principle of the subordinate following the predominant.

Core Idea
In purchase, the whole rules; in eating, each part counts.

The passage deals with a nuanced area of oaths (ayman) where an object contains a mix of what is forbidden and what is permitted by the oath. The Hanafi school introduces a key distinction: the nature of the action (buying vs. eating) determines whether the majority or the individual components govern the ruling. This lesson dissects that distinction and its underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the oath? Buying a bunch of dates. Ruling: no hanth (no violation).
2
Identify the condition
The bunch contains a small amount of ripe dates. The ruling hinges on the amount being little.
3
Identify the proof
The illa: purchase encounters the whole (jumla) and the minor is subordinate to the predominant (al-maghluub tabi' li al-ghaalib).
4
Contrast the ruling for eating
If the oath were on eating, it would break because each piece is encountered alone.
5
Summarize the principle
The distinction is based on the nature of the action: purchase considers the composite, eating considers the individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يحنث بشراء كباسة بسر فيها رطب قليل، لم يحنث؛ لأن الشراء يصادف الجملة والمغلوب تابع للغالب، وقيد بالشراء؛ لأنه إذا كان اليمين على الأكل يحنث؛ لما ذكرنا أن الأكل يصادفه وحده [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٥/١] (مستخلص) رطباً لأن القليل تابع للكثير ، ولو كان اليمين على الأكل يحنث. (ط، عيني)
And he does not break the oath by buying a bunch of unripe dates which has a little fresh dates in it, he does not break the oath because purchase encounters the whole and the subordinate is a follower of the predominant. And it is qualified with purchase because if the oath was on eating, he breaks the oath due to what we mentioned that eating encounters it alone. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/355) (Mukhtasar) Fresh dates because the little is a follower of the much. And if the oath was on eating, he breaks it. (T, Ayni)
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning an oath not to buy fresh dates. If one buys a bunch of unripe dates that contains a small amount of fresh dates, he does not break his oath. The reason (illa) is that a purchase transaction deals with the item as a whole, and the minor component is considered subordinate to the dominant one (al-maghluub tabi' li al-ghaalib). This is contrasted with an oath regarding eating: if he swore not to eat fresh dates and ate a bunch containing both, the oath would be broken because each piece is consumed individually, and the fresh date is encountered alone. The commentary notes that this distinction is based on the principle that the little follows the much in purchase but not in eating.

Key Terms
كباسة (kabasa): A bunch of dates; like a cluster from a palm tree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to buy fresh dates and buys a bunch of unripe dates that contains a few fresh dates, according to Hanafi fiqh, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because fresh dates are present.
B) No, because the bunch is predominantly unripe.
C) Yes, because the fresh dates are noticeable.
D) No, because he intended unripe dates.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, for oaths involving composite objects, the nature of the action (purchase vs. eating) determines whether the majority governs: purchase considers the whole, eating considers each part.

Oath Without Pointing: Effect of Transfer of Ownership
Ruling on oaths without pointing and effect of ownership transfer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when someone swears not to eat someone's food without pointing, and then the owner sells it, and the oath-taker eats it after transfer.

Core Idea
An oath on property without pointing refers to the specific property at the time of oath; sale ends the oath's scope.

When someone swears an oath without using a pointing word like 'this', the oath attaches to the property as it exists in the owner's possession at the moment of swearing. If the owner later transfers that property (e.g., sells the food), the oath no longer applies to it. This lesson walks through the basic scenario and the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its wording
The oath is 'I will not eat the food of Zayd' without pointing. Determine that there is no demonstrative pronoun restricting the object.
The absence of 'this' makes the oath general but attached to Zayd's ownership.
2
Determine the event: property leaves Zayd's ownership
Zayd sells the food. The food is no longer owned by Zayd at the time the oath-taker eats it.
3
Apply the principle: oath follows ownership at time of action
The oath was contracted on an action (eating) occurring in a place (food) added to Zayd. Since Zayd no longer owns it, the action does not fulfill the conditions of the oath.
The ruling: no violation (la yahnath).
4
Contrast with pointing case
If he had said 'this food', the oath would be restricted to that specific item regardless of ownership changes.
Pointing restricts; absence keeps the oath attached to the owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يشر إلخ أي لو قال: "لا يأكل طعام فلان" و لم يشر أي لم يقل لفظة "هذا" بعد المضاف إليه في الأمثلة المذكورة، فباع فلان الطعام وأخواته، ثم أكله الحالف بعد زوال الملك لا يحنث ؛ لأنه انعقد يمينه على فعل واقع في محل مضاف إلى فلان ولم يوجد فلا يحنث، وقوله: "وحنث بالمتجدد" أي إذا حلف لا يدخل دار فلان، فباعها فلان واشترى داراً أخرى فدخلها الحالف، حنث؛ لأن يمينه انعقدت على فعل واقع في محل مضاف إلى فلان وقد وجد وكذا الحكم في أخواتها وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يحنث في الملك المتجدد له في الدار وحدها؛ لأن الملك لا يستحدث فيها عادة، فهي أول ما يشترى وآخر ما يباع فتقيدت اليمين المضافة إلى الدار بالقائمة في ملكه وقت اليمين وهما يقولان إن اللفظ مطلق، فيجري على إطلاقه، وعنه في رواية: ينعقد اليمين في الجميع بالقائم في ملكه وقت الحلف. (مستخلص، فتح)
And if he did not point... meaning if he said, 'I will not eat the food of Zayd' and did not point—i.e., did not say the word 'this' after the genitive in the mentioned examples—then if Zayd sold the food and its like, and then the oath-taker ate it after the transfer of ownership, he does not break his oath; because his oath was contracted upon an action occurring in a place added to Zayd, and it did not occur, so he does not break his oath. And his statement: 'And he breaks his oath with the newly acquired' means if he swore not to enter Zayd's house, then Zayd sold it and bought another house, and the oath-taker entered it, he breaks his oath; because his oath was contracted upon an action occurring in a place added to Zayd, and it occurred. The same ruling applies to its parallels. And from Abu Yusuf: he does not break his oath regarding the newly acquired property for him specifically in the house alone; because ownership is not customarily acquired newly in a house, as it is the first thing bought and the last thing sold, so the oath added to the house is restricted to what existed in his ownership at the time of the oath. And they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say the wording is absolute, so it runs on its absoluteness. And from him in a narration: The oath is contracted in all cases upon what exists in his ownership at the time of the oath. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for oaths made without a pointing word like 'this' (hadha). The principle: if the oath is added to something belonging to someone (e.g., 'food of Zayd'), it refers to the specific property in that person's ownership at the time of the oath. If Zayd sells the food and the oath-taker eats it after the sale, there is no violation because the oath was on Zayd's property, which has now left his ownership. However, if Zayd acquires new property after the oath (e.g., buys a new house), entering that new house does violate the oath because the description 'Zayd's house' applies to any house he owns. Abu Yusuf makes an exception for houses: since houses are typically owned continuously (first bought, last sold), the oath is restricted to the house owned at the time of the oath. But the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the absolute wording applies to any house acquired afterward. A narration from Abu Yusuf aligns with them. This is an intra-Hanafi difference with practical consequences: for food and movable property, the oath follows the property; for houses, Abu Yusuf restricts it.

Key Terms
المشار (al-mushār): That which is pointed to by a demonstrative pronoun like 'this' (hadha).
المتجدد (al-mutajaddid): Newly acquired property that meets the description in the oath after the oath was taken.
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Pointing by a demonstrative pronoun to a specific object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'I will not eat the bread of Ali' without pointing. Ali sells the bread to a baker. The man later buys and eats that bread from the baker. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the bread originally belonged to Ali.
B) No, because the bread left Ali's ownership before eating.
C) Yes, because the oath is on any bread that was once Ali's.
D) No, because the oath was invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
An oath without pointing on property is tied to that property being in the specified owner's possession at the time the action occurs; transfer of ownership nullifies the oath's application.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Newly Acquired Property and the House Exception
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths and newly acquired property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Yusuf's exception for houses and evaluate the majority position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf restricts oaths on houses to the specific house; the majority applies absolute wording to any house subsequently acquired.

The passage introduces a dispute within the Hanafi school about oaths that refer to newly acquired property. While the general rule is that an absolute oath applies to any property that fits the description after the oath, Abu Yusuf makes an exception for houses. This lesson analyses the reasoning behind this exception and the majority response.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for newly acquired property
If someone swears not to enter Zayd's house without pointing, and Zayd later sells that house and buys another, entering the new house breaks the oath because the description 'Zayd's house' applies to any house he owns.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the majority).
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's exception
Abu Yusuf holds that for houses only, the oath is restricted to the house owned at the time of the oath. He argues that houses are not customarily 'newly acquired' in the same way as movables; they are typically owned continuously.
Exception applies only to houses, not to food, clothes, etc.
3
Understand the evidence for each side
Majority: the wording is absolute (muthlaq), so it should be given full effect. Abu Yusuf: custom (‘urf) indicates that houses are not usually replaced frequently, so the oath is contextually limited.
The text also reports a narration where Abu Yusuf agrees with the majority.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position is the majority view: violation with newly acquired property, including houses. This is because the absolute wording is given effect and custom is not strong enough to override it.
The text cites later sources (Mustakhlas, Fath) indicating this is the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يشر إلخ أي لو قال: "لا يأكل طعام فلان" و لم يشر أي لم يقل لفظة "هذا" بعد المضاف إليه في الأمثلة المذكورة، فباع فلان الطعام وأخواته، ثم أكله الحالف بعد زوال الملك لا يحنث ؛ لأنه انعقد يمينه على فعل واقع في محل مضاف إلى فلان ولم يوجد فلا يحنث، وقوله: "وحنث بالمتجدد" أي إذا حلف لا يدخل دار فلان، فباعها فلان واشترى داراً أخرى فدخلها الحالف، حنث؛ لأن يمينه انعقدت على فعل واقع في محل مضاف إلى فلان وقد وجد وكذا الحكم في أخواتها وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يحنث في الملك المتجدد له في الدار وحدها؛ لأن الملك لا يستحدث فيها عادة، فهي أول ما يشترى وآخر ما يباع فتقيدت اليمين المضافة إلى الدار بالقائمة في ملكه وقت اليمين وهما يقولان إن اللفظ مطلق، فيجري على إطلاقه، وعنه في رواية: ينعقد اليمين في الجميع بالقائم في ملكه وقت الحلف. (مستخلص، فتح)
And if he did not point... meaning if he said, 'I will not eat the food of Zayd' and did not point—i.e., did not say the word 'this' after the genitive in the mentioned examples—then if Zayd sold the food and its like, and then the oath-taker ate it after the transfer of ownership, he does not break his oath; because his oath was contracted upon an action occurring in a place added to Zayd, and it did not occur, so he does not break his oath. And his statement: 'And he breaks his oath with the newly acquired' means if he swore not to enter Zayd's house, then Zayd sold it and bought another house, and the oath-taker entered it, he breaks his oath; because his oath was contracted upon an action occurring in a place added to Zayd, and it occurred. The same ruling applies to its parallels. And from Abu Yusuf: he does not break his oath regarding the newly acquired property for him specifically in the house alone; because ownership is not customarily acquired newly in a house, as it is the first thing bought and the last thing sold, so the oath added to the house is restricted to what existed in his ownership at the time of the oath. And they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say the wording is absolute, so it runs on its absoluteness. And from him in a narration: The oath is contracted in all cases upon what exists in his ownership at the time of the oath. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for oaths made without a pointing word like 'this' (hadha). The principle: if the oath is added to something belonging to someone (e.g., 'food of Zayd'), it refers to the specific property in that person's ownership at the time of the oath. If Zayd sells the food and the oath-taker eats it after the sale, there is no violation because the oath was on Zayd's property, which has now left his ownership. However, if Zayd acquires new property after the oath (e.g., buys a new house), entering that new house does violate the oath because the description 'Zayd's house' applies to any house he owns. Abu Yusuf makes an exception for houses: since houses are typically owned continuously (first bought, last sold), the oath is restricted to the house owned at the time of the oath. But the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the absolute wording applies to any house acquired afterward. A narration from Abu Yusuf aligns with them. This is an intra-Hanafi difference with practical consequences: for food and movable property, the oath follows the property; for houses, Abu Yusuf restricts it.

Key Terms
المشار (al-mushār): That which is pointed to by a demonstrative pronoun like 'this' (hadha).
المتجدد (al-mutajaddid): Newly acquired property that meets the description in the oath after the oath was taken.
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Pointing by a demonstrative pronoun to a specific object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'I will not wear any of Khalid's shirts' without pointing. Khalid sells his shirts and buys new ones. According to the mufta bihi, does wearing the new shirt break the oath?
A) Yes, because the wording is absolute and applies to any shirt owned by Khalid.
B) No, because shirts are like houses and Abu Yusuf's exception applies.
C) Yes, but only if the shirt is the same brand.
D) No, because the oath was made before the purchase of new shirts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether oaths without pointing cover newly acquired houses; the operative ruling follows the majority that they do, due to the absolute wording.

Oath about Owner of Specific Garment After Sale
Ruling on oath about owner of a garment after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi consensus rule regarding oaths tied to owners of specific items and apply it to new scenarios.

Core Idea
An oath about an owner remains binding even after the item is sold, because the intent is the person, not the property.

This lesson examines a concise ruling on oaths that refer to the owner of a specific item, such as a garment or a house. The key question: if the owner sells the item, does the oath still apply? The Hanafi school holds, by consensus, that the oath remains binding because the avoidance is directed at the person's essence, not at the incidental property. Understanding this principle prevents the mistaken belief that transferring ownership can nullify an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Consider a person who swears, 'I will not speak to the owner of this cloak.' The oath is validly made.
2
Examine the ownership change
Later, the owner sells the cloak. The item is no longer his property.
3
Determine the ruling
The question: If the oath-taker now speaks to the former owner, does he break his oath? According to Hanafi consensus (ijma), he does break the oath (yanthiru).
4
Understand the rationale
The rationale is that the oath-taker's avoidance is for the person himself (al-dhat), not for the item. The item is merely a descriptor, not the cause of avoidance.
5
Generalize the principle
The same ruling applies to any similar oath, e.g., 'owner of this house' or 'owner of this garment.' The identity of the item does not affect the oath's binding nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي لو حلف لا يكلم صاحب هذا الطيلسان فباعه فكلمه حنث. والظاهر أن الطيلسان مثال، فإن قوله: صاحب هذه الدار أو صاحب هذا الثوب كذلك؛ لأن الإنسان لا يعادي لأجل الدار وغيرها، بل لمعنى فيه فيراد الذات. (فتح، مستخلص) فباعه: أي باع صاحب الطيلسان طيلسانه . (عيني) حنث إجماعاً لأن الامتناع لذاته لا للطيلسان. (عيني)
Meaning if he swore not to speak to the owner of this taylasan (cloak), and he sold it, then he spoke to him, he breaks his oath by consensus. And it is apparent that the taylasan is an example; for his saying 'owner of this house' or 'owner of this garment' is similar; because a person is not hostile for the sake of the house or other things, but rather due to a meaning in him, so the essence is intended. (Fath, Mustakhlas) He sold it: meaning the owner of the taylasan sold his taylasan. (Ayni) He breaks his oath by consensus because the abstention is for his own essence, not for the taylasan. (Ayni)

Commentary
This ruling addresses oaths referring to the owner of a specific item, like 'owner of this cloak'. Even if the person sells that item, the oath remains binding—this is by consensus. The reason is that the oath-taker avoids the person himself, not because of the item. For example, if someone swears not to speak to 'the owner of this house', and that person sells the house, the oath still applies. The item is merely incidental; the hostility is toward the person. This ruling also applies if someone swears not to speak to 'the owner of this garment' or similar. The key is that the avoidance is for the person's own sake, not for the property.

Key Terms
الطيلسان (al-ṭaylasān): A type of cloak worn over the turban.
الذات (al-dhāt): The essence or person of the oath-taker's target.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because the avoidance is for the owner himself.
B) No, because he is no longer the owner of that car.
C) Yes, because the oath is tied to the car.
D) No, because the oath becomes invalid after the sale.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi consensus: an oath about the owner of a specific item remains binding even after the item is sold, because the oath is directed at the person, not the property.

Classification of Actions in Oaths: Direct vs Agency
Classifies actions under oaths into two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify actions into two categories for the purpose of oath-breaking and apply the classification to determine whether an oath is broken by direct action or by an agent's action.

Core Idea
An oath is broken by direct action in sales, but by direct or agency action in marriages — rights determine the rule.

Oaths often involve actions that can be performed by the swearer or by an agent. Hanafi fiqh draws a crucial distinction based on the nature of the action: some actions attach legal rights to the performer (agent), others to the principal. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq classifies common actions and explains the reasoning, forming a foundational principle for oath-breaking rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides actions into those that break the oath only by direct performance (mubasharah) and those that break the oath both by direct performance and by the agent's action.
Memorize the examples: sale, purchase, lease (first category); marriage, divorce, manumission (second category).
2
Analyze the first category – rights to the agent
For actions like sale, the rights and consequences of the contract belong to the person who actually performs it. Therefore, if the swearer appoints an agent, the agent is the contractor, and the swearer does not break the oath.
The oath condition is that the swearer does it himself; agency does not satisfy this.
3
Analyze the second category – rights to the principal
For actions like marriage, the legal rights (e.g., marital rights) return to the principal, and the agent is merely a messenger. Hence, the oath is broken both by direct action and by the agent's action.
The agent's action is attributed to the principal in effect.
4
Examine exceptions and special cases
If the swearer intends to forbid command, or if he is a ruler (sultan) who does not personally handle such matters, the oath may be broken by the agent's action even in the first category.
Intention and customary practice can modify the ruling.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the agent's action never breaks the oath (in either category). The Hanafi school argues that this overlooks the attribution of rights.
The practical difference is significant in cases involving delegated acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(مص)] أي الأفعال التي يحنث فيها الحالف بإتيانها بنفسه لا بأمره للغير هو البيع والشراء وغيرهما، والحاصل أن الفعل على وجهين: الأول: يرجع حقوقه إلى العاقد، ففي مثل هذا الفعل يحنث بفعله بنفسه، ولا يحنث بمباشرة المأمور، والثاني: أن يرجع حقوقه إلى المأمور، ثم ينتقل منه إلى الآمر، الأول البيع والشراء ومعطوفاتهما، فلو حلف لا يبيع أو لا يشتري أو لا يؤاجر أو لا يستأجر أو لا يصالح مال أو لا يقاسم مع الشركاء أو لا يخاصم فلاناً أو لا يضرب ولده، ففي هذه الصور لو فعل هذه الأفعال بنفسه يحنث، ولو وكل غيره ففعل ذلك لم يحنث ؛ لأن صدور هذه الأشياء من الوكيل، فلم يوجد شرط الحنث الحالف، وهو الإتيان بنفسه، الثلاثة: يحنث إلا عن الشافعي في الأظهر لا يحنث، نعم! لو نوى بحلفه أن لا يأمر به غيره يحنث بالأمر أيضاً؛ لأن فيه تشديداً، وكذا يحنث إذا كان الحالف ذا سلطان لا يتولى هذه الأمور بنفسه؛ لأنه يمنع نفسه عما يعتاده، فيحنث بفعل المأمور، وإنما قيد الصلح عن مال احترازاً عن الصلح عن دم عمد، فإنه من القسم الثاني كما يأتي. (مستخلص، عيني، محشي) من وعند لا بالأمر: أي لا يحنث فيه بالأمر منه لغيره إذا كان ممن يباشر بنفسه. (ط) عيني) البيع: خبر المبتدأ مع معطوفاته، ومنه الهبة بعوض (ط) الولد أي الكبير ذكراً كان أو أنثى. Chapter of oath in sale, purchase, and marriage etc., i.e., in explaining their rulings and hajj, prayer, fasting and others like walking, wearing, sitting. What makes one break the oath by direct action not by command: sale, purchase, lease, hire, settlement for property, division, lawsuit, and beating a child, and what makes one break by both. Restricted to being for acknowledgment. From, and of, i.e., every action in which... What makes one break [relative clause, i.e., every action. (Mas)] i.e., the actions in which the oath-breaker breaks by doing them himself and not by his command to others are sale and purchase and others. The summary: action is of two types: first: its rights return to the contractor, so in such an action he breaks by doing it himself, and he does not break by the agent's direct action. Second: its rights return to the agent, then transfer to the principal. The first: sale and purchase and their conjunctions. So if he swore not to sell or not to buy or not to lease or not to hire or not to settle property or not to divide with partners or not to sue so-and-so or not to beat his child, then in these cases if he did these actions himself he breaks the oath, and if he appointed an agent and he did that, he does not break, because these things issued from the agent, so the condition of breaking for the swearer, which is doing it himself, is not found. The three: he breaks except from al-Shafi'i in the apparent view he does not break. Yes! If he intended by his oath that he not command others, he breaks by command as well, because it includes intensity. And likewise he breaks if the swearer is a ruler who does not handle these matters himself, because he prevents himself from what he is accustomed to, so he breaks by the agent's action. And he restricted settlement for property to avoid settlement for intentional homicide, for it is of the second type as will come. (Mustakhlas, Ayni, Muhashshi) From, and according to, not by command: i.e., he does not break by command from him to others if he is one who does it himself. (T., Ayni) Sale: predicate of the subject with its conjunctions, and from it gift with compensation (T.) The child i.e., the adult, male or female. (Ayni) And what makes one break by both: i.e., the actions in which the swearer breaks by doing them himself and by command to others, i.e., agency, are marriage, divorce, and their conjunctions. And the meaning of marriage is getting married, not giving in marriage, for that is the first type. So if he swore not to marry or not to divorce his wife or not to divorce her by khul' or not to free his slave or not to make kitabah or not to settle for intentional homicide or not to give a gift or not to give charity or not to give a loan or not to take a loan or not to beat his slave or not to slaughter a sheep or not to build a building or not to sew his garment or not to deposit or not to receive a deposit or not to lend or not to borrow or not to pay a debt or not to receive a debt or not to clothe so-and-so or not to carry this thing, he breaks both by doing it himself and by his agent's action, because the agent in these things is a messenger and transmitter and the rights of the contract return to the principal, not to the agent, contrary to the first section, for the rights of the contract therein return to the contractor. And according to al-Shafi'i: he does not break by the agent's action, because the action exists from him in reality and from the other in effect, so the condition of breaking exists from the principal in one way and not in another, so he does not break as in the first type.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage establishes a fundamental classification of actions in the context of oaths. The Hanafi school divides actions into two categories: (1) Those where the legal rights and consequences attach to the person performing the action (the agent). In such actions, the oath is only broken when the swearer performs the action himself. Examples include sale, purchase, lease, etc. (2) Those where the legal rights attach to the principal even if an agent performs the action. In these, the oath is broken both by direct action and by appointing an agent. Examples include marriage, divorce, manumission, etc. The reasoning is that in the first category, the agent is the real party to the contract, while in the second, the agent is merely a messenger and the rights flow to the principal. The text also notes exceptions: if the swearer intends to forbid command, or if he is a ruler who typically acts through agents, then the oath may be broken by the agent's action. Finally, the Shafi'i position is mentioned as holding that the oath is not broken by the agent's action in either category, but the Hanafi position is preferred.

Key Terms
مباشرة (mubasharah): Direct performance of an action by oneself, without an agent.
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed to perform an act on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears not to sell anything, and then his agent sells an item, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because selling through an agent is still selling
B) No, because the oath is only broken by direct action
C) It depends on his intention
D) Only if he intended to forbid agency

Key Takeaway
Oaths over actions are broken by direct action for sales etc., but by both direct and agency actions for marriages etc., based on where legal rights attach.

Oaths Conditional on Sale with Khiyar al-Shart
Ruling on an oath conditional on sale or purchase with an option clause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an oath of sale triggers manumission only when sold with khiyar al-shart, and apply the ruling to similar contractual scenarios.

Core Idea
An oath conditional on sale only breaks when ownership remains—sale with khiyar keeps ownership.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, as explained by Badr al-Din al-'Ayni, tackles a subtle point in Hanafi fiqh: when someone swears an oath like 'If I sell this slave, he is free,' the slave becomes free only if the sale leaves ownership with the seller. A final sale transfers ownership and dissolves the oath; a sale with an option (khiyar al-shart) does not. Let's break down the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and Its Condition
The oath is either 'If I sell this slave, he is free' or 'If I buy this slave, he is free'. The condition (sale or purchase) triggers the penalty (manumission).
The slave is the subject of the oath's consequence.
2
Distinguish Between Final Sale and Sale with Khiyar
A final sale (bat) transfers ownership completely, so the condition is fulfilled without the penalty (the oath is dissolved). A sale with khiyar al-shart does not transfer ownership fully; the seller remains owner during the option period.
This is the key distinction: ownership must remain at the moment the condition occurs.
3
Apply the Principle: 'Al-Mu'allaq Ka al-Munajjaz'
The suspended (ta'liq) is treated like the immediate (tanjiz). If a person immediately freed a slave after a purchase with khiyar, the option would be cancelled and the manumission valid. So when the freedom is suspended on a sale with khiyar, it also becomes valid when the sale occurs.
This istihsan-based analogy is the proof for the ruling.
4
Note Exceptions and Other Contract Types
The same ruling applies to fasid (invalid) and mawquf (suspended) contracts, because ownership remains. It does not apply to batil (void) contracts, which are legally non-existent.
Khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) do not prevent ownership transfer, so they do not trigger the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن بعته إلخ: أي لو قال رجل: إن بعت هذا العبد فهو حر، أو قال: إن اشتريت هذا العبد فهو حر، فعقد الحالف بشرط الخيار يحنث، فيعتق العبد، وقيد بالخيار؛ لأنه لو قال: إن بعته فهو حر، فباعه بيعاً صحيحاً بلا خيار لا يعتق؛ لزوال ملكه وتنحل اليمين؛ لتحقق الشرط، ويحنث بشرط الخيار ؛ لوجود الشرط وهو البيع والشراء مع قيام الملك عند الإمام؛ إذ المبيع لا يخرج عن ملك البائع باشتراط الخيار له اتفاقاً، وخيار المشتري وإن منع من دخوله في ملك المشتري عنده لكن العتق معلق بتعليقه، والمعلق كالمنجز، ولو نجز العتق بعد الشراء بالخيار انفسخ الخيار، ووقع العتق فكذا إذا علق، وقيدنا بخيار الشرط؛ لأن خيار العيب والرؤية لا يمنع زوال المبيع عن ملك البائع، وشرط الخيار للبائع مانع من زواله عن ملكه. (فتح عيني) فعقد: أي البائع في الأول والمشتري في الثاني. بالخيار حنث، وكذا بالفاسد ،والموقوف لا بالباطل. إن لم أبع فكذا فأعتق أي بشرط الخيار أي الحالف ذلك العبد حنث فيعتق العبد لوجود شرط العتق وهو البيع أو الشراء، ولقيام الملك عند وجود الشرط. (عيني)
If I sell it, etc.: That is, if a man says: 'If I sell this slave, he is free,' or says: 'If I buy this slave, he is free,' and the swearer makes a contract with a condition of option (khiyar), he breaks the oath, and the slave is freed. It is restricted to khiyar because if he said: 'If I sell him he is free,' and he sold him with a valid sale without option, he does not become free, because his ownership is removed and the oath is dissolved since the condition is fulfilled. But he breaks the oath with khiyar because of the existence of the condition (sale/purchase) while ownership remains according to Imam Abu Hanifa, since the sold item does not leave the seller's ownership by stipulating khiyar for him, by agreement. And the buyer's option, although it prevents entry into the buyer's ownership according to him, but the manumission is suspended on its suspension, and the suspended is like the immediate (munajjaz). If immediate manumission occurred after a purchase with option, the option would be cancelled and the manumission would take effect, so likewise when suspended. We restricted it to khiyar al-shart (stipulated option) because khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) do not prevent the sold item from leaving the seller's ownership. And khiyar al-shart for the seller prevents it from leaving his ownership. (Fath al-'Ayni) 'He contract': i.e., the seller in the first case, and the buyer in the second. With khiyar, he breaks the oath, and likewise with fasid (invalid) and suspended (mawquf), but not with batil (void). If I do not sell, then so-and-so is free' meaning with khiyar, i.e., the swearer of that slave breaks the oath and the slave is freed because the condition for freedom (sale or purchase) is met, and ownership remains at the time of the condition. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when someone swears an oath saying 'If I sell this slave, he is free' or 'If I buy this slave, he is free.' The key point is that the oath is broken (the slave is freed) only if the sale or purchase is made with a condition of option (khiyar al-shart). If the sale is final (bat) without any option, the slave is not freed because ownership passes completely and the oath's condition is fulfilled without the need for the penalty (the slave's freedom). However, with a valid khiyar, the ownership does not fully transfer—the seller remains owner during the option period—so the condition of sale is met while the seller still owns the slave, triggering the penalty. The text distinguishes khiyar al-shart from other types of options (defect, sight) which do not prevent transfer of ownership. The commentary then notes that 'with khiyar he breaks the oath' and contrasts it with fasid and mawquf contracts (which also trigger the oath) but not batil contracts. The last part discusses a different scenario: if he says 'If I do not sell so-and-so, then...' and then manumits or contracts a sale that makes the slave no longer sellable, the oath is broken because the condition (not selling) becomes impossible to fulfill.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulated option in a sale where the seller or buyer has the right to cancel within a specified period; ownership does not transfer until the option expires.
المعلق كالمنجز (al-mu'allaq ka al-munajjaz): A legal principle: a suspended (conditional) act is treated like an immediate act for legal purposes, unless the immediate act would be invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'If I sell this slave, he is free', when is the sale considered to have occurred for the purpose of breaking the oath?
A) Only if the sale is final without option.
B) Only if the sale includes a condition of option (khiyar al-shart).
C) Any valid sale, with or without option.
D) Only if the sale is invalid (fasid).

Key Takeaway
In an oath conditional on sale, the penalty is triggered only if the sale leaves ownership intact, as with khiyar al-shart, fasid, or mawquf contracts, but not with a final valid sale or batil contract.

6 min
Oaths with Fasid Contracts
Ruling on oaths broken by invalid sales or purchases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a fasid sale or purchase breaks an oath, and apply the conditions of possession and liability to determine the outcome in specific scenarios.

Core Idea
A fasid contract transfers ownership upon possession, breaking an oath on sale or purchase.

This lesson examines the effect of fasid (voidable) contracts on oaths. While a fasid contract is defective, it still effects a transfer of ownership when possession is taken, which triggers the condition of an oath. The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary by Fath al-Qadir present detailed scenarios involving a slave to illustrate when the oath is broken. We will also explore a dissenting view from Abu Yusuf and the majority position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The matn states that a fasid sale or purchase breaks the oath. Note this as the default ruling.
‌
2
Analyze the Seller Scenario
If the swearer is the seller, examine whether the slave is in the buyer's hand with liability (by pledge, trust, or usurpation) or remains in the seller's hand. If in buyer's hand with liability, ownership passes and the oath dissolves (slave not freed); if in seller's hand, ownership does not pass and the oath breaks (slave is freed).
Liability means the buyer bears risk.
3
Analyze the Buyer Scenario
If the swearer is the buyer, the fasid purchase enters his ownership only if he takes possession and assumes liability. If so, the oath breaks and the slave is freed; otherwise not.
Ownership is determined by possession and liability.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that a fasid contract does not break the oath unless it includes an option (khiyar). The majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and others) disagree.
The text says 'wa bihi qalat al-thalathah' indicating the majority view.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is the majority view: fasid contracts break oaths with the detailed conditions on possession.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا بالفاسد: أي لو حلف لا أو لا يشتري وباع بيعاً فاسداً أو اشترى شراء فاسداً يحنث، فإن كان الحالف يبيع هو الحالف، فصورة من بعتقه هو البائع ينظر: فإن كان العبد في يد المشتري بحيث يكون ضمانه على المشتري بمثل رهن أو أمانة أو غصب لا يعتق؛ لأنه كما يتم البيع يزول عن ملكه كالبيع الصحيح ،البات وينبغي أن ينحل اليمين، وإن كان العبد في يد البائع عتق؛ لأنه لا يزول ملكه قبل التسليم إلى المشتري، وإن كان الحالف بعتقه هو المشتري فاشتراه فاسداً، فإن كان في يده مضمونا عليه يعتق لدخوله في ملكه كما تم البيع، وإلا فلا يعتق؛ لعدم ملكه، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يحنث بالفاسد، وإلا بما فيه خيار لأحدهما أصلاً، وبه قالت الثلاثة. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٨/١] (فتح)
And likewise with fasid: that is, if he swears not to sell or not to buy, and he sells a fasid sale or buys a fasid purchase, he breaks the oath. Then if the swearer who frees is the seller, the scenario: if the slave is in the buyer's hand such that liability is on the buyer like by pledge, trust, or usurpation, he is not freed because as the sale is completed, it leaves his ownership like a valid final sale, and the oath should dissolve. But if the slave is in the seller's hand, he is freed, because his ownership does not leave before delivery to the buyer. And if the swearer who frees is the buyer, and he buys a fasid purchase, then if it is in his hand and liable upon him, he is freed because it enters his ownership as the sale is completed; otherwise, he is not freed due to lack of ownership. And from Abu Yusuf: that he does not break the oath with fasid, except what has a khiyar for one of them originally, and the three (Imams) said that. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/368] (Fath)
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage deals with oaths involving invalid (fasid) contracts. The general rule is that a fasid sale or purchase does break the oath because it still effects some transfer of ownership and is considered a valid contract in terms of triggering the condition. However, the commentary from Fath al-'Ayni provides a detailed scenario: If the swearer is the seller and the slave (object of oath) is already in the buyer's possession with liability, the slave is not freed because ownership passes, so the oath is dissolved. If the slave remains in the seller's possession, the slave is freed because ownership has not passed. For the buyer, if he takes possession and becomes liable, ownership is established and he breaks the oath; otherwise not. A dissenting view from Abu Yusuf is mentioned: he holds that a fasid contract does not break the oath unless it includes an option clause. The relied-upon position (al-thalathah) agrees with the general rule.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A voidable sale that is defective in its conditions but still transfers ownership if possession is taken and liability assumed; e.g., selling something not owned but later approved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a seller makes a fasid sale and the slave (object of oath) is already in the buyer's hand as a pledge (rah)?
A) The seller breaks the oath and the slave is freed.
B) The oath is dissolved because ownership passes to the buyer, so the slave is not freed.
C) The oath is not broken because fasid does not count.
D) The oath is broken only if the slave is returned to the seller.

Key Takeaway
A fasid contract breaks an oath on sale or purchase if possession and liability transfer ownership, with the majority view prevailing over Abu Yusuf's dissenting opinion.

Oaths and Contract Validity: Mawquf vs Batil
Distinguishing mawquf and batil in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mawquf and batil contracts and apply the Hanafi rule that only legally recognized contracts break an oath.

Core Idea
A suspended contract breaks the oath; a void contract does not — the latter is no sale in law.

This passage sharpens our understanding of how oaths interact with contract types. We already know that selling or buying can trigger an oath. Now we learn that only contracts with some legal standing — like suspended (mawquf) or defective (fasid) — actually break it. A utterly void (batil) contract, like selling carrion, is a nullity and leaves the oath intact. The text also provides a crisp Hanafi definition of batil vs fasid and handles special cases like slaves and free persons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contract categories
Recognize that the passage contrasts 'mawquf' (suspended) and 'batil' (void) in relation to oaths. Mawquf contracts, like a fuduli sale, are pending approval and considered valid in potential.
Fasid (voidable) contracts, by implication, also break the oath, as they are not batil.
2
Understand why mawquf breaks the oath
A suspended sale is a real sale that will become effective upon approval. Therefore, the condition in the oath is fulfilled when the sale occurs, even if it is not yet finalized.
Both the seller and buyer scenarios are covered.
3
Grasp why batil does not break the oath
A batil sale is not a sale in reality or legal effect. It does not confer any rulings of sale, even if possession is transferred. Hence, the oath condition is not met.
The text explicitly states 'because it is not a sale or purchase in reality or in effect.'
4
Apply the Hanafi batil vs fasid distinction
If one countervalue is not property in any revealed religion (e.g., carrion, blood, free person), the sale is batil. If it is property in some religions but the transaction is impermissible (e.g., slave for wine), it is fasid and breaks the oath.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
5
Analyze special cases and the intra-madhhab difference
Buying a mudabbar or umm walad normally does not break the oath, but a judge's ruling can change that. For a free woman or umm walad, Abu Yusuf holds the contract valid; the majority considers it batil.
Recognize that the majority view is the mufta bihi, but Abu Yusuf's opinion is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموقوف : أي وكذا يحنث بالبيع والشراء الموقوفين في المسألة السابقة، فإن كان البائع البيع الموقوف أن يقول: إن بعت عبد فلان فعبدي "حر" فباع عبد فلان بغير إجازته عتق عبد الحالف لوجود الشرط، وإن كان الحالف هو المشتري فصورته أن يقول: "إن اشتريت عبداً فهو . حر" فاشتری. عبداً فضولي حنث بالشراء. (عيني، فتح) لا بالباطل أي لو قال له: "إن بعته أو ابتعته فهو حر، فعقد البيع الباطل أو الشراء الباطل بأن باعه بالميتة أو اشتراه بها لا يحنث؛ لأنه ليس ببيع وشراء حقيقة ولا حكماً . لا يفيد شيئاً من حتى أحكام البيع والشراء وإن اتصل به القبض والفرق بين الباطل والفاسد أن أحد العوضين إذا لم يكن مالاً في دين سماوي فالبيع باطل كبيع الميتة والدم والحر، وكذا البيع بها. وإن كان في بعض الأديان مالاً وأمكن اعتباره مثمناً فالبيع فاسد كبيع العبد بالخمر، وإن تعين كونه مبيعاً فباطل أيضاً كبيع الخمر بالدراهم، أو الدراهم بالخمر، ولو اشترى مدبراً أو أم ولد لا يحنث ولو قضى القاضي بجوازه يحنث للحال؛ لأن قضاءه يؤثر في إزالة المانع من الجواز، فيقتصر على وقت القضاء فيحنث حينئذ، والمكاتب كالمدبر في رواية، ولو حلف أن هذا الحر فباعه بر؛ لأن البيع الصحيح لا يتصور فيه فانعقد على الباطل، وكذا لو عقد يمينه على الحرة أو أم الولد، وعن أبي يوسف في الحرة وأم الولد ينعقد على الصحيح. (عيني، فتح)
And the suspended (mawquf): that is, likewise he breaks the oath with suspended sale and purchase in the previous issue. If the seller of the suspended sale says: 'If I sell the slave of so-and-so, then my slave is free' and he sells the slave of so-and-so without his permission, then the slave of the swearer is freed because the condition exists. And if the swearer is the buyer, his scenario is to say: 'If I buy a slave, he is free' and he buys a slave by a fuduli (unauthorized agent) contract, he breaks the oath by the purchase. ('Ayni, Fath) Not with batil: that is, if he says to him: 'If I sell it or buy it, he is free,' and he makes a batil sale or batil purchase, such as selling it for carrion or buying it with carrion, he does not break the oath, because it is not a sale or purchase in reality or in effect. It does not confer any of the rulings of sale and purchase even if delivery is connected. The difference between batil and fasid is that if one of the two countervalues is not considered property in a revealed religion, the sale is batil, like selling carrion, blood, or a free person; likewise selling for them. And if it is considered property in some religions and can be treated as valuable, the sale is fasid, like selling a slave for wine. And if it is determined to be an object of sale, it is also batil, like selling wine for dirhams or dirhams for wine. And if he buys a mudabbar or umm walad, he does not break the oath; but if the judge rules its permissibility, he breaks the oath immediately, because his ruling affects removal of the impediment to permissibility, so it is limited to the time of judgment, and he breaks the oath then. The mukatab is like the mudabbar in one narration. And if he swears that this free person is... and sells him, it is invalid because a valid sale is not conceivable in that case, so it is concluded on batil. Likewise if he makes his oath on a free woman or umm walad. And from Abu Yusuf regarding the free woman and umm walad: it is concluded on the valid. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on which contracts break an oath. Suspended (mawquf) contracts, such as a sale by a fuduli (unauthorized agent) that requires approval, do break the oath because they are considered valid contracts in potential. If the seller is the swearer and sells something he does not own, the condition is triggered. If the buyer is the swearer, buying via fuduli also triggers the oath. However, absolutely void (batil) contracts do not break the oath because they are not legally recognized as sales at all. The text then defines the difference between batil and fasid: batil when the subject matter is not legally valuable (like carrion, blood, free person), fasid when it is valuable but impermissible for other reasons (like wine). Specific cases: buying a mudabbar (slave promised freedom after master's death) or umm walad does not break the oath because they cannot be sold normally; but if a judge permits it, then it breaks the oath. A mukatab (slave contracted for freedom) is like a mudabbar. If the oath is on a free person (e.g., 'If I sell this free man'), the contract is batil and does not break the oath. Abu Yusuf has a different view for free women and umm walad, considering the contract valid.

Key Terms
البيع الباطل (al-bay' al-batil): A void sale that does not transfer ownership and has no legal effect, e.g., selling carrion or a free person.
البيع الموقوف (al-bay' al-mawquf): A suspended sale, e.g., by a fuduli, that becomes effective upon approval.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A voidable sale that is defective but may be valid if the defect is removed, e.g., selling a slave for wine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of contract does NOT break an oath of sale or purchase?
A) Fasid (voidable)
B) Mawquf (suspended)
C) Batil (void)
D) All of the above break the oath

Key Takeaway
Only contracts with legal standing (mawquf, fasid) trigger an oath; utterly void (batil) contracts have no effect.

Oath on Condition of Not Selling After Manumission
Ruling on an oath conditioned on not selling when sale becomes impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an oath conditioned on not selling is followed by an act that makes sale impossible, and explain the Hanafi rational proof that the oath is broken.

Core Idea
Removing the ability to sell after swearing not to sell breaks the oath immediately.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a person swears that if he does not sell a particular slave, his wife is divorced. He then manumits the slave or makes him mudabbar, thereby making the sale impossible. The Hanafi ruling is that the wife is divorced because the condition of not selling is fulfilled. The passage also addresses objections based on speculative possibilities, showing that rulings are based on current reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The actor swears an oath conditioned on not selling a slave. He then manumits or makes mudabbar. The ruling: the wife is divorced (the oath is broken).
2
Locate the proof
The proof is rational: the condition is the absence of sale; when sale becomes impossible, the condition is realized. Loss of salability is the operative factor.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to objections
The text refutes the argument that a mudabbar can still be sold with a judge's permission, and that an umm walad could theoretically become a slave again. The response: such possibilities are too remote; rulings are based on current realities.
4
Note the authority weight
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن لم أبع فكذا: أي من يبيع قال: إن لم أبع هذا العبد فامرأته طالق فأعتق العبد أو دبره طلقت امرأته وحنث في يمينه؛ لأن الشرط هو عدم البيع وقد تحقق؛ لفوات المحلية، فتحقق العجز عن البيع، فإن قيل: المحلية في التدبير باقية فإنه يمكن بيع المدبر إذا قضى القاضي بجوازه، قلنا: عند القضاء بجوازه ينفسخ التدبير وفوات المحلية إنما كان باعتبار بقاء التدبير والحكم لا يبتني على ما يظهر عند قضاء القاضي في المجتهدات، ولا يقال: لم يقع اليأس من بيع أم الولد بجواز أن ترتد وتلحق بدار الحرب ثم تسبى وتسترق؛ لأنا نقول: الحالف عقد يمينه على البيع باعتبار هذا الملك، وذلك لا يمكن بعد هذه التصرفات؛ ولأن هذه الحالات موهومات والأحكام لا تبتني على الموهومات، فتحقق اليأس عن البيع، والمراد من قوله: "أو "دبر" هو التدبير المطلق؛ لأنه يمتنع به البيع. (مستخلص، فتح)
'If I do not sell, then so-and-so': that is, one who sells says: 'If I do not sell this slave, then my wife is divorced.' Then he manumits the slave or makes him mudabbar, his wife is divorced and he breaks his oath, because the condition is the absence of sale, and it has been realized due to the loss of the subject's salability. Thus, inability to sell is realized. If it is said: the salability in tadbir remains, because a mudabbar can be sold if the judge rules its permissibility, we say: when the judge rules its permissibility, the tadbir is annulled, and the loss of salability was only by virtue of the continuation of tadbir, and the ruling is not based on what appears at the judge's ruling in matters of ijtihad. And it is not said: despair of selling umm walad has not occurred because she could apostatize, join the enemy, be captured, and enslaved; for we say: the swearer made his oath on sale based on this ownership, and that is not possible after these actions, and because these possibilities are imaginary, and rulings are not based on imaginary things. So despair of sale is realized. And the meaning of his saying 'or mudabbar' is absolute tadbir, because it prevents sale. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where someone swears, 'If I do not sell this slave, my wife is divorced,' and then he manumits the slave or makes him mudabbar (promises freedom after his death). The ruling is that his wife is divorced because the condition (not selling) is fulfilled—the slave can no longer be sold due to manumission or tadbir. The text addresses potential objections: one might argue that a mudabbar can still be sold if a judge permits it, but the response is that the sale would then annul the tadbir; the oath is based on the current state, not speculative future possibilities. Similarly, for umm walad (mother of a child), although theoretically she could become a slave again if she apostatizes and is captured, such possibilities are too remote to consider. The oath is considered broken because the immediate salability is lost.

Key Terms
التدبير (al-tadbir): The act of declaring that a slave will be free upon the master's death; it prevents the slave from being sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'If I do not sell this slave, my wife is divorced' and then manumits the slave, what happens?
A) The wife is not divorced because the slave could still be sold.
B) The wife is divorced immediately because the slave can no longer be sold.
C) The oath is canceled because manumission overrides it.
D) The wife is divorced only if the manumission is by a judge's order.

Key Takeaway
When an oath is conditioned on not selling, any act that makes sale impossible breaks the oath immediately.

Fulfilling an Oath with Defective Coins
Ruling on repaying debt with defective coins in oath fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repaying a debt with defective coins in fulfillment of an oath and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
Repaying with defective but genuine dirhams fulfills an oath because species outweighs defect.

When someone swears to repay a debt in dirhams, what if they pay with coins that are slightly debased or even later claimed by another? The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes coins that still qualify as dirhams from those that do not. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind accepting defective coins for oath fulfillment and contrasts it with Imam Malik's stricter view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: repaying with ziyuf, nuhrija, or mustahaqqa coins fulfills the oath and avoids breaking it.
These coins are still considered dirhams in species.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that Imam Malik holds that such repayment breaks the oath.
The khilaf is on whether defect negates the species.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Malik's basis: defective coins are not proper fulfillment because they are not sound dirhams.
No explicit dalil is given in the text; likely based on a stricter interpretation of 'dirham'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that ziyuf, nuhrija, and mustahaqqa retain the genus (jins) of dirhams. Defect does not remove the species. Acceptance is valid, and return after acceptance does not annul fulfillment.
This is one of five issues where ziyuf are treated like good coins.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Malik, the swearer breaks the oath and must pay kaffarah; under Hanafi, no breakage.
Practical consequence: which school you follow affects whether expiation is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليقضين دينه اليوم فقضاه زيوفا أو بنهرجة أو مستحقة بر، ولو رصاصاً أو ستوقة لا. أي لو حلف فلان ليقضين دين فلان، فقضى الحالف الدين زيوفاً، مأخوذ من زافت عليه الدراهم أي صارت مردودة عليه بغش فيها وقيل: هو ما دون البنهرجة في الردة؛ لأن الزيف ما يرده بيت المال، وقيل ما رب من الدراهم في غير دار الضرب وفي غير دار السلطان ولا يرده التجار، أو قضى الحالف بنهرجة معرب بنهره، والبهر الحظ والنون للنفي يعني حظ هذه الدراهم من الفضة أقل، وغشه أكثر، وتردها التجار يعني المستقصي منهم، ويقبلها المسهل منهم، وهو الزيف، أو قضاه دراهم مستحقة من بفتح الحاء وهي التي استحقها مستحق بعد القضاء. بر أردأ وقوله: أي في حلفه ولا يحنث؛ لأن الزيف دراهم حقيقة غير أن فيه عيباً، لا وهو يعدم الجنسية، ولهذا لو تجوز به صار مستوفياً، وكذلك البنهرجة، وقبض المستحق صحيح حتى لو أجازه المستحق في الصرف والسلم بعد الافتراق جاز، وعند مالك لله يحنث في جميع ذلك، وهذه المسألة إحدى المسائل الخمس التي جعلوا الزيوف فيها كالجياد. والحاصل أن قبض المستحقة صحيح، ولا يرتفع برده البر بعد ما تحقق؛ إذ الرد بعد القبض الصحيح لا يعدم القبض بل ينقصه واليمين قد انحلت قبل النقص بالرد، فقضاء هذه الأصناف من الدراهم، فوجد شرط البر، فلا يحنث . (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) زيوفاً: أي حال كونه زيوفاً وهو جمع زيف وهو ما يأخذه التجار ويرده بيت المال ويكون الغش فيه قليلاً. (ط،ع) أو ستوقة لا : [ أي وقضاه ستوقة بفتح السين المهملة وتشديد التاء التي وسطها رصاص وهي بالفارسية ستاقه، أي الحالف في يمينه؛ لأن الرصاص والستوقة ليسا من جنس الدراهم. (عيني)] عطف على قوله: "لو رصاصاً" أي لو قضى الحالف في حلفه ليقضين دينه رصاصاً أو ستوقة لا يبر في يمينه والستوقة أردأ من البنهرجة، وعن الكرخي: الستوقة . عندهم ما كان الصفر أو النحاس هو الغالب الأكثر، وفي "اليوسفية": البنهرجة إذا غلبها النحاس لم تؤخذ، وأما الستوقة فحرام أخذها؛ لأنها فلوس وعليه أن يتقي الله إذا رضي بأخذها، فلا يعطيها لغيره بلا بيان، وقيد الستوقة بالغالب من الصفر أو النحاس؛ لأنه لو كان أكثر فضة والأقل ستوقة لا يحنث ؛ لأن العبرة للغالب، ووجه عدم البر في لا يبر • الرصاص والستوقة أنهما ليسا من جنس الدراهم، ولو تجوز بهما في الصرف والسلم لا يجوز. (فتح عيني)
He repays his debt today, then he repays it with ziyuf (defective coins), or with nuhrija (debased coins), or with mustahaqqa (coins claimed by rightful owner), he has fulfilled (the oath). And if with lead or satuqa (copper/tin with lead), he has not fulfilled. That is, if a man swore to repay so-and-so's debt, and the swearer repaid the debt with ziyuf coins. 'Ziyuf' is derived from 'zaafat alayhi al-darahim' meaning they were returned to him due to adulteration. It is said: it is what is below nuhrija in rejection; because ziyuf is what the treasury returns. It is also said: it is what is minted outside the mint and outside the sultan's authority, and merchants do not reject it. Or the swearer repaid with nuhrija, which is a Persianized word meaning 'without luck' (be-nahre), i.e., the luck (share of silver) in these coins is less, and the adulteration is more, and exacting merchants reject them but lenient ones accept them, and it is like ziyuf. Or he repaid with mustahaqqa (claimed) coins, with fathah on the ha, which are coins that a rightful claimant claimed after the repayment. 'Barra' means he fulfilled. And his statement: i.e., in his oath, he does not break his oath because ziyuf are truly dirhams, except that they have a defect, but they lack not the species. Hence, if one used them as a gift, he would be considered as having fulfilled. Similarly for nuhrija, and accepting mustahaqqa is valid, so much so that if the owner validates it in a currency exchange or salam contract after separating, it is valid. According to Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, he breaks his oath in all of these. And this issue is one of the five issues in which they considered ziyuf as good coins. The conclusion is that accepting mustahaqqa is valid, and its return does not negate the fulfillment after it has occurred, because returning after a valid acceptance does not nullify the acceptance but rather reduces it, and the oath has already been dissolved before the reduction by return. So repayment with these types of dirhams fulfills the condition of fulfillment, so he does not break his oath. (Mukhallis, Fath, 'Ayni) 'Ziyufan': i.e., being ziyuf, plural of zayf, which merchants take and the treasury rejects, and its adulteration is slight. (Tatawun, 'Ayni) Or satooq no: [i.e., if he repaid with satooq (with fat-h on seen and tashdid on ta, which has lead in its middle, Persian 'sataqah'), i.e., the swearer in his oath; because lead and satooq are not of the genus of dirhams. ('Ayni)] It is a conjunct to his statement: 'if with lead,' that is, if the swearer in his oath to repay his debt repays with lead or satooq, he does not fulfill his oath. And satooq is worse than nuhrija. From al-Karkhi: satooq according to them is when brass or copper is the predominant majority. In al-Yusufiyyah: nuhrija if copper dominates is not taken, but satooq is prohibited to take because it is fals (copper coins). And he must fear Allah if he accepts it, so he should not give it to others without disclosure. The restriction of satooq to the predomination of brass or copper is because if it were mostly silver with a small amount of satooq, he does not break his oath because consideration is to the dominant. The reason for not fulfilling with lead and satooq is that they are not from the genus of dirhams, and if one uses them in currency exchange or salam, it is not valid. (Fath, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This is a detailed ruling on oaths to repay debts, specifically regarding the quality of coins used for repayment. The Hanafi position is that repaying with defective but still legally recognized dirhams (ziyuf, nuhrija, mustahaqqa) constitutes valid repayment that fulfills the oath and avoids breakage. The reasoning is that these coins are still considered dirhams in specie, even if defective. However, paying with lead (rusas) or satuqa (a base metal coin with lead or copper) does not fulfill the oath because these are not dirhams at all. The text provides linguistic and juristic definitions: ziyuf are coins that merchants accept but the treasury rejects, with a small amount of adulteration; nuhrija are worse, with less silver; mustahaqqa are coins that are later claimed by a rightful owner. Imam Malik holds the opposite view, that even these defective coins break the oath. The Hanafi school rejects this, basing their view on the principle that the species (jins) is maintained despite defects. The text also notes a nuance: if the defective coin is predominantly silver, it counts as dirhams; if the base metal dominates, it does not. This is one of five issues where ziyuf are treated like good coins. The commentary emphasizes that acceptance of such coins is valid, and returning them after acceptance does not nullify the fulfillment.

Key Terms
زيوف (ziyuf): Defective coins with a slight admixture of alloy, accepted by merchants but rejected by the treasury.
بنهرجة (nuhrija): Debased coins with a higher alloy content than ziyuf, rejected by exacting merchants but accepted by lenient ones.
ستوقة (satuqa): Coins made primarily of lead or copper with a small amount of silver, not considered dirhams.
مستحقة (mustahaqqa): Coins that are valid at time of payment but later claimed by a rightful owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man swears to repay a debt in dirhams and pays with nuhrija coins (debased but silver), does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because nuhrija are not real dirhams.
B) No, because nuhrija are still considered dirhams despite the defect.
C) Only if the creditor rejects them.
D) It depends on the amount of alloy.

Key Takeaway
Repaying a debt with defective but genuine dirhams fulfills an oath; only coins that are not dirhams (lead, satuqa) break it.
