Buying Cloth by the Cubit: Three Hanafi Views
Intra-Hanafi dispute over sale of cloth by cubit when length varies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi imams' rulings on excess/deficiency in cubit-based cloth sales and explain the talil (rationale) behind each position.

Core Idea
A cubit can be treated as a description, a separate unit, or a measure — each yields a different ruling on price and khiyar.

When a cloth is sold by the cubit at a fixed price per cubit, what happens if the actual length differs? The three eponymous Hanafi imams gave different answers, each based on how they conceptualize the 'cubit' in the contract. In this lesson, we unpack their positions and the subtle legal reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Articulate the contract scenario
Identify the key terms: cloth sold as 'ten cubits, each cubit for one dirham'. The buyer receives a piece that is longer or shorter.
2
State Abu Hanifa's view and talil
Abu Hanifa: if longer, take for ten dirhams, no khiyar; if shorter, pay nine dirhams and have khiyar. His reason: the cubit is a description (wasf), not the essence; excess is a free bonus, deficiency reduces price proportionally.
He analogizes to a garment described with a certain length.
3
State Abu Yusuf's view and talil
Abu Yusuf: if longer, pay eleven dirhams and have khiyar; if shorter, pay full ten dirhams and have khiyar. His reason: each cubit is like a separate cloth; excess must be paid, deficiency does not reduce the price, but buyer has option due to harm.
He treats each cubit as an independent unit of sale.
4
State Muhammad's view and talil
Muhammad: if longer, pay ten and a half dirhams and have khiyar; if shorter, pay nine and a half and have khiyar. His reason: each cubit is analogous to a measure (kayl); price is apportioned exactly, and option exists to prevent harm.
He draws an analogy to commodities sold by weight.
5
Compare practical outcomes
Note the thamarah al-khilaf: the amount paid and whether the buyer has a right of cancellation. Also note the exception for coarse cloth (karbas) where the excess must be paid according to all.
This shows how different usul frameworks can produce divergent fiqh rulings within the same school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اشترى ثوباً على أنه عشرة أذرع كل ذراع بدرهم فوجده عشرة ونصفاً أخذه بعشرة ولا خيار له ولو وجده تسعة ونصفاً أخذه بتسعة وله الخيار. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف: يأخذه في الأول بأحد عشر وفي الثاني بعشرة ويخير في الوجهين. وقال محمد: يأخذه في الأول بعشرة ونصف وفي الثاني بتسعة ونصف ويخير فيهما؛ لأنه لما سمى لكل ذراع ثمناً على حدة التحق كل ذراع بالكيل، ومن ضرورة مقابلة الذراع بالدرهم مقابلة النصف بالنصف، والخيار يثبت له لئلا يتضرر. ولأبي يوسف: أنه لما قابل كل ذراع بدرهم صار كل ذراع كثوب على حدة، وقد نقص فلا يسقط شيء من الثمن ثم يخير. ولأبي حنيفة: أن الذراع فيه وصف في الأصل. وقيل: هذه الأقوال في الثوب الذي تتفاوت جوانبه، وأما في الكرباس فلا تسلم له الزيادة. (عيني، فتح)
And whoever buys a cloth on condition that it is ten cubits each cubit for a dirham, and finds it ten and a half, he takes it for ten and has no option; and if he finds it nine and a half, he takes it for nine and has the option. This is with Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: he takes it in the first case for eleven and in the second for ten, and he has option in both. Muhammad said: he takes it in the first for ten and a half and in the second for nine and a half, and he has option in both; because when he specified a price for each cubit separately, each cubit becomes analogous to a measure (kayl), and it is necessary to match the cubit with the dirham half by half, and option is established for him to avoid harm. For Abu Yusuf: because he matched each cubit with a dirham, each cubit becomes like a separate cloth, and if it is deficient, nothing of the price is omitted, then he has option. For Abu Hanifa: the cubit is originally a description. It is said: these statements are for a cloth whose sides vary (like a garment); as for coarse cloth (karbas), the excess is not given to him.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. It discusses a scenario where a buyer purchases a cloth on the condition that it measures ten cubits, with each cubit priced at one dirham. When the buyer receives it, the length may be more or less. The three eponymous Hanafi imams differ in their rulings. Abu Hanifa considers the cubit as a description (wasf) and the price as a lump sum; thus, any excess is a bonus for the buyer without additional payment (no khiyar in increase), and any deficiency reduces the price proportionally with khiyar. Abu Yusuf treats each cubit as an independent unit like separate pieces of cloth; thus, in excess, the buyer pays for the extra cubit at contract price, and in deficiency, the full price is owed (no reduction) but he has khiyar. Muhammad treats the cubit as a measure (kayl) like a weight, so price is apportioned exactly: excess requires extra payment and deficiency reduces price, with khiyar in both due to harm. The text adds that these views apply to tailored cloth (like a qamis) where partial cutting harms it; for coarse cloth (karbas) that can be cut without harm, the customer does not get the excess free. This illustrates the Hanafi methodology of analogizing (qiyas) to different principles (description vs. separate item vs. measure).

Key Terms
الوصف (al-wasf): Description; here means the cubit is a qualifying attribute, not a measure.
الكيل (al-kayl): Measure; here Muhammad treats each cubit as a measured unit, requiring exact proportionality.
الكرباس (al-karbas): Coarse cloth that is not harmed by cutting; the exception where excess must be paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a cloth sold as 10 cubits at 1 dirham per cubit turns out to be 9.5 cubits, what does the buyer pay and what option does he have?
A) Pays 10 dirhams, no option.
B) Pays 9 dirhams, with option to cancel.
C) Pays 9 dirhams, no option.
D) Pays 9.5 dirhams, with option.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the same transaction can be viewed through different conceptual lenses (description, separate unit, measure) leading to divergent rulings, all of which are valid within the school.

What Enters the Sale of a House: Taba'iyyah and Locks
Ruling on items included in house sale by default

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between items included and excluded from the sale of a house by default, and explain the Hanafi principle of taba'iyyah (subordination).

Core Idea
Attached items follow the house; separate accessories require explicit mention.

When you buy a house, do you automatically get the keys? The answer is not simply 'yes'—it depends on whether the keys belong to fixed locks or portable padlocks. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on what enters the sale of a house without separate mention, based on the key principle of taba'iyyah (subordination) and a Prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
The passage states that the building (bina') and keys (mafatih) enter the sale of a house by default. Note that 'keys' are qualified: only keys for fixed locks (aghlaq), not padlocks (aqfal).
The default inclusion covers the structure and its permanently attached fittings.
2
Extract the distinction between aghlaq and aqfal
Aghlaq are locks that are attached to the door (e.g., bolts and plates). Aqfal are portable padlocks. Keys for aghlaq are included; keys for aqfal are not, by consensus (ijma').
The attachment to the building is the decisive factor.
3
Understand the general principle (taba'iyyah)
Anything physically attached to the house (muttasil) enters the sale as a follower (taba'). Examples: attached stairs, lower millstone built in, well and pulley, even a large garden within the property. Such items have no separate share of the price unless explicitly allocated.
Taba'iyyah is a core Hanafi concept in sales: the subordinate follows the principal without additional consideration.
4
Analyze the role of qiyas and istihsan
In qiyas, keys would not enter because they are neither part of the named item (the house) nor attached. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan: keys are followers of locks because neither is useful without the other. This overrides the qiyas ruling.
Istihsan here is based on custom and necessity (ma'ruf and hajah).
5
Evaluate the hadith proof for the building
The hadith 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it up to the sky and what is below it to the depths' proves that the building (above) and foundations (below) are owned with the land. This is a dalil naqli with qat'i dalala but zanni thubut in this context.
The hadith is cited to justify inclusion of the structure, not the keys; keys are justified by istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: يدخل البناء والمفاتيح في بيع الدار، أي العمارة. والمفاتيح: مفاتيح الأغلاق لا مفاتيح الأقفال، ويدخل ما كان متصلاً بالبناء. إذا كانت المفاتيح والأغلاق من الخشب أي المتصل بالباب، وإذا كانت من الحديد غير متصل به لا يدخل بالإجماع. والحاصل أن المتصل بالدار يكون تبعاً لها، ويدخل في البيع كالسلم المتصل وحجر الرحى الأسفل المبنية في الدار والبئر وبكرتها، وكذا البستان الذي فيها ولو كبيراً. فإذا كان الغلق متصلاً بالباب ومركباً فيها مثل الكيلون والضبة فتدخل المفاتيح، وإن لم يكن الغلق مركباً فيها كالقفل لا يدخل، ولا المفاتيح. وفي القياس لا تدخل المفاتيح؛ لأنها ليست من مسمى المبيع، ولا هي متصلة به. وجه الاستحسان: أن المفاتيح تبع للأغلاق؛ إذ لا ينتفع بكل واحد منهما بدون الآخر. ثم اعلم أن ما يدخل في البيع تبعاً لم يكن له حصة من الثمن إلا بالتسمية. ودخول البناء في بيع الدار لقوله عليه السلام: "من ملك أرضاً ملك ما فوقها إلى السماء، وما تحتها من الثرى". (عيني، فتح، معدن)
Chapter: What enters the sale. The building and keys enter the sale of a house, meaning the structure. Keys mean keys of locks (aghlaq), not keys of padlocks (aqfal). What is attached to the building enters. If the keys and locks are wooden, i.e., attached to the door; if they are iron and not attached, they do not enter by consensus. The summary: what is attached to the house is a follower (taba') and enters the sale, like the attached stairs, the lower millstone built into the house, the well and its pulley, and likewise the garden within it, even if large. If the lock is attached to the door and fixed into it, like a bolt (kaylun) and a plate (dabba), then the keys enter; if the lock is not fixed like a padlock (qufl), it does not enter, nor do the keys. In qiyas, keys would not enter because they are not part of the named item nor attached. The basis of istihsan: keys are followers of locks, as neither functions without the other. Know that what enters the sale as a follower has no share of the price unless specified. The building enters the sale of a house because of the hadith: 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it up to the sky and what is below it to the depths.'
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It begins a new chapter discussing what is included in the sale of a house without explicit mention. The author states that the building (bina') and keys (mafatih) are included. However, there is a distinction: keys attached to the building as part of the locks are included; separate keys for padlocks are not. The general principle is that anything physically attached to the house (muttasil) follows the sale as a tabi' (subordinate). Examples include attached staircases, millstones, wells, and even a large garden within the property. The key point is that the lock must be fixed to the door; then the keys follow by istihsan (juristic preference) because neither is useful without the other. The proof for the building itself is a hadith: ownership of land includes everything above and below. This passage illustrates the Hanafi concept of taba'iyyah (subordination) in sales.

Key Terms
الأغلاق (al-aghlaq): Locks that are attached to the door, as opposed to portable padlocks.
الأقفال (al-aqfal): Portable padlocks not attached to the structure.
تبعاً (taba'an): As a follower; items included in sale without separate price because they serve the main object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Keys for all locks.
B) Keys for padlocks only.
C) Keys for locks attached to the building.
D) No keys are included.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales, attached items follow the house; keys are included only when they belong to fixed locks, due to custom and necessity (istihsan).

Buyer's Option for Extra or Deficient Cloth Length
States the buyer's option when cloth length differs from specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the buyer's option when the delivered cloth length differs from the agreed specification, and apply it to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Extra length benefits the buyer; deficiency harms — the option arises only from harm.

When you order a specific length of cloth and receive something different, does Islamic law give you a choice to cancel the sale? The answer depends on whether the difference is a pure benefit or a loss. This lesson from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the simple but precise rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage considers two cases: (1) cloth found to be ten and a half cubits when ten was specified, and (2) cloth found to be nine and a half cubits when ten was specified.
The half-cubit difference is the only delta mentioned.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For the extra half-cubit, the buyer has no option (بلا خيار) and must take the cloth, receiving the extra length free. For the deficient half-cubit, the buyer has an option (بخيار) to accept with a price reduction (implicitly) or reject the sale.
The passage explicitly says 'بلا خيار' and 'بخيار'.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Extra length is a pure benefit without harming the buyer, so no option is needed. Deficiency harms the buyer by depriving him of the agreed measurement, so an option is granted to restore balance.
This reflects the Hanafi doctrine that khiyar is based on harm (darar).
4
Apply the rule to other slight differences
If the extra or deficiency is minimal (like a quarter cubit), the same reasoning applies: extra is accepted, deficiency triggers option. However, larger deviations may change the ruling (e.g., if cloth is half the length, sale is void).
The passage only covers a half-cubit difference; the principle extends by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في عشرة ونصف بلا خيار، وبتسعة في تسعة ونصف بخيار. (عيني، فتح) في عشرة ونصف أي في حال كونه وجده عشرة أذرع ونصف ذراع (عيني) بلا خيار للمشتري، فيسلم له نصف ذراع مجانا. (عيني) في تسعة ونصف أي في حال كونه وجد الثوب تسعة أذرع مع زيادة نصف ذراع، ولكن يأخذه إن شاء وهو معنى قوله: بخيار. (عيني)
In [a case of finding] ten and a half [cubits when ten was specified], there is no option; and in [a case of finding] nine in nine and a half, there is an option. (Ayni, Fath) In ten and a half, meaning in the case where he finds it ten cubits and a half cubit (Ayni), there is no option for the buyer, so he is given the half cubit for free. (Ayni) In nine and a half, meaning in the case where he finds the cloth nine cubits with an increase of half a cubit, but he may take it if he wishes, which is the meaning of his saying: with option. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq gives a concise ruling on a sale of cloth measured by the cubit. The ruling is that if the buyer finds the cloth to be longer than specified (e.g., ten and a half cubits when ten was agreed), he must accept it without any option to reject because the extra length is pure benefit. If the cloth is shorter (e.g., nine and a half cubits instead of ten), he has the option to accept it at the agreed price or reject it entirely due to the deficiency. The commentary by al-Ayni and al-Fath explains the reasoning: no harm in the extra length, but the shortfall deprives the buyer of a desired attribute.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): Option or right to annul the sale due to a defect or deficiency
ذراع (dhira'): A unit of length, cubit, used for measuring cloth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer has the option to accept or reject.
B) The buyer must accept and receives the extra half cubit free.
C) The buyer must accept and pays extra for the half cubit.
D) The sale is void due to the discrepancy.

Key Takeaway
Extra length is a benefit the buyer must accept without option; deficiency gives the buyer an option to cancel or keep with compensation.

The Principle of Ittisal Qarar and Automatic Inclusion of Keys
Explains the principle of automatic inclusion of appurtenances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ittisal qarar to determine whether keys and locks are automatically included in a house sale.

Core Idea
Keys follow locks only if the lock is fixed to the door; the rule is connection for stability, not mere proximity.

When you buy a house, does the key automatically come with it? The Hanafi school says it depends on how the lock is attached. This lesson unpacks the key concept of 'connection for stability' (ittisal qarar) and its application to keys, stairs, wells, and other appurtenances. You will learn why a built-in lock and its key are automatically included, but a padlock and its key are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sold item and its appurtenances
First, determine what is the primary object of the sale (e.g., house, land). Then list items that might be considered part of it (keys, stairs, wells).
2
Apply the ittisal qarar test
Check whether the item is permanently attached to the sold property for stability (ittisal qarar). If yes, it enters the sale without mention. If not, it does not – unless custom dictates otherwise.
For keys: the key is not attached, but it follows the lock if the lock is fixed. This is an exception by istihsan.
3
Distinguish between fixed and non-fixed locks
A lock that is built into the door (like a bolt or a latch) is considered attached; its key is a dependent. A separate padlock is not attached, so neither the lock nor its key enters.
Hanafis rely on custom (urf) and necessity (the key is useless without the lock) to include keys of fixed locks.
4
Expand to other attached structures
Apply the same test to stairs, lower millstone, well, pulley, and garden – all are attached for stability and therefore enter the sale. The hadith 'whoever owns land owns what is above it' supports this.
The hadith is cited to justify buildings being included in the sale of land.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط، مستخلص) مي في بيع الدار الأن في العرف يطلق اسم الدار على العرصة والبناء (عيني) أي فيما إذا كانت المفاتيح والأغلاق من الخشب أي المتصل بالباب وإذا كانت من الحديد غير متصل به لا يدخل بالإجماع، والحاصل أن المتصل بالدار يكون تبعاً لها، ويدخل في البيع كالسلم المتصل وحجر الرحى الأسفل المبنية في الدار والبئر وبكرتها، وكذا البستان الذي فيها ولو كبيراً، فإذا كان الغلق متصلاً بالباب ومركباً فيها مثل الكيلون والضبة، وهما الذهب العريض أو الفضة العريضة يجعل على وجه الباب، فتدخل المفاتيح لم يكن الغلق مركباً فيها كالقفل لا يدخل؛ لعدم الاتصال، ولا المفاتيح، وفي القياس لا تدخل المفاتيح؛ لأنها تبعاً، وإن ليس من مسمى المبيع، ولا هي وجه الاستحسان: أن المفاتيح تبع للأغلاق؛ إذ لا ينتفع بكل واحد منهما بدون الآخر، ثم اعلم أن ما يدخل في البيع تبعاً لم يكن له حصة من الثمن إلا بالتسمية، ودخول البناء في بيع الدار؛ لقوله عليه: "من ملك أرضاً ملك ما فوقها إلى السماء، وما تحتها من الثرى". (عيني، معدن، فتح)  كتاب البيوع ٢٥٧ ما يدخل في البيع والشجر في بيع الأرض بلا ذكر، ولا يدخل الزرع في بيع الأرض بلا تسمية أي ويدخل الشجر ولا الثمر في بيع الشجر إلا بالشرط، ويقال للبائع: أي ولا يدخل الثمر في الصورتين والشجر إلخ: أي تبعاً مطلقاً ولو صغيراً أو غير مثمر، إلا أن يكون يابساً؛ لأنها بعد اليبس حطب لا شجر، وقوله: "بلا ذكر" يتعلق بقوله: "يدخل" في المسألتين، أي بلا ذكر هذه الأشياء في البيع، والأصل في هذا الباب أن كل شيء متصل بالمبيع اتصال قرار يدخل في البيع، وإلا فلا إلا إذا جرى العرف بالدخول فيه كالمفاتيح والسلم المتصل بالبناء يدخل ولو كان من خشب، وغير المتصل لا يدخل رمز الحقائق: ۷/۲، ۸] (فتح) بلا ذكر: راجع إلى المسألتين؛ لأنه متصل بها للقرار فصار كالبناء. (عيني) الزرع إلخ: لأنه متصل بها للفصل من الآدمي، فلا يرد حمل الجارية؛ لأن ذلك بفصل الله، ولو كان الزرع مغيباً في الأرض من الكراث يدخل في البيع المطلق على الصحيح؛ لأنه يبقى سنين بمنزلة الشجر لا ما كان ظاهراً، والورد وورق التوت والآس ونحوها كالثمار. تتمة: ثياب الغلام والجارية تدخل في البيع بغير شرط للعرف إلا أن تكون ثيابا فاخرة تلبس عند العرض على البيع لا يدخل بدون شرط وخطام البعير والحبل عنق الحمار، والبرذعة والإكاف يدخل للعرف، بخلاف السرج، وولد الناقة والرمكة والأتان والبقر والمعز إن ذهب به مع الأم إلى موضع البيع دخل فيه للعرف، وإلا فلا. (عيني، فتح بلا تسمية إلا إذا ينبت ولا قيمة له فيدخل في الأصح، وكذا إذا لم ينبت بعد. (عيني) ولا الثمر إلخ لأن اتصال الزرع والثمر ليس للقرار، فصارا كالمتاع الموضوع في الدار، وعبر ههنا بـ"الشرط" وفي الزرع بـ"التسمية؛ ليفيد عدم الفرق، وأن هذا الشرط غير مفسد وخصه بالثمر اتباعا لقوله عليه: "من اشترى أرضا فيها نخل فالثمرة للبائع إلا أن يشترط المبتاع من غير فصل بين المؤبر وغيره، ويقوم مقام التسمية، والشرط ما لو قال: "بكل قليل وكثير هو فيها" إلا أن يبينه بقوله : من حقوقها أو من مرافقها؛ لأن الحق يذكر منه كالطريق والشرب والمرافق ما يرتفق به كمسيل الماء. " هو تبع لابد للمبيع . والتأبير: أن يشق وعاء نحل أنثى، فيجعل فيه شيء من طلع نخل ذكر وهو إصلاح للثمر ، وعند الثلاثة: لو لم يكن الثمرة مؤبرة تدخل، وإلا فلا وفي القياس يدخل الزرع والثمر، وكان وجه القياس بالنظر لمطلق الاتصال بالمبيع، وإن لم يكن على وجه القرار. (عيني، فتح) بالشرط: لأن اتصالهما للقرار فصار كالمتاع الموضوع في الدار. ويقال للبائع إلخ: أي بعد نقد المشتري الثمن : اقطع الزرع والثمرة، وسلم الأرض والشجرة؛ لأن ملك المشتري مشغول بملك البائع، فكان عليه تفريغه وتسليمه، كما إذا كان فيه متاع موضوع، وقال الشافعي: يترك حتى يظهر صلاح الثمرة - أي ينتفع به الدواب أو الإنسان - ويستحصد الزرع؛ لأن الواجب هو التسليم المعتاد، وفي العادة أن لا يقطع كذلك، وصار كما إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة وفي الأرض زرع لم يدرك، فإنه يترك إلى الحصاد، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا أن التسليم واجب بحكم العقد، بخلاف الإجارة؛ لأنها للانتفاع، فيترك بأجر المثل، =
Section: On explaining what enters the sale without mention and what does not enter, and other things. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 7/2] And al-mafatih: plural of miftah, keys of locks not keys of padlocks, and what is connected to the building enters. (T, Mustakhlas) Meem-Ya: In a sale of a house now, in custom the name 'house' applies to the vacant land and the building (Ayni) meaning when the keys and locks are of wood, i.e., connected to the door, and if they are of iron not connected to it, it does not enter by consensus. The gist is that what is connected to the house is a dependent and enters the sale, like the attached stairs and the lower millstone built in the house, and the well and its pulley, and likewise the garden that is in it even if large. So if the lock is connected to the door and fixed in it like the kilon and dabbah, which are broad gold or broad silver placed on the face of the door, then [their] keys enter; if the lock is not fixed in it like a padlock, it does not enter due to lack of connection, nor the keys. And by qiyas the keys would not enter because they are not part of the named sold item, nor are they connected. The basis of istihsan is that keys are dependent on locks, since neither can be used without the other. Then know that what enters the sale as a dependent does not have a share of the price unless specified. And the entry of the building in the sale of a house is due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it to the sky and what is below it to the depths.' (Ayni, Madan, Fath) [Page 258] The Book of Sales. 257. What enters the sale. And trees [enter] in the sale of land without mention, and crops do not enter in the sale of land without specification, i.e., trees enter, and fruit does not enter in the sale of trees except by condition, and it is said to the seller: i.e., fruit does not enter in both cases. And 'trees etc.': i.e., absolutely as a dependent, even if small or non-fruit-bearing, unless it is dry, because after dryness it is firewood, not trees. And his statement 'without mention' relates to his statement 'enters' in both issues, i.e., without mention of these things in the sale. The principle in this chapter is that everything connected to the sold item with a connection of stability enters the sale, otherwise not unless custom includes it, like keys and stairs attached to the building enter even if of wood, and what is not attached does not enter. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 7/2, 8) (Fath) 'without mention': refers to both issues because it is connected to them for stability, so it becomes like the building. (Ayni) 'Crops etc.': because they are connected to them for separation by humans, so it does not apply to the pregnancy of a slave girl because that is by God's separation. And if the crop is buried in the ground like leeks, it enters in an absolute sale according to the correct view, because it remains for years like trees, unlike what is above ground. And roses, mulberry leaves, myrtle, and the like are like fruits. Completion: The clothes of a slave boy and slave girl enter the sale without condition because of custom, unless they are fine clothes worn for display at the time of sale, in which case they do not enter without condition. And the nose-ring of a camel, the rope around a donkey's neck, the packsaddle and the saddle blanket enter because of custom, unlike the saddle. And the offspring of a she-camel, a white mare, a female donkey, a cow, and a goat, if taken with the mother to the place of sale, enter because of custom, otherwise not. (Ayni, Fath) Without specification unless it is growing and has no value, then it enters according to the most correct; likewise if it has not yet grown. (Ayni) 'And not fruit etc.' because the connection of crops and fruit is not for stability, so they become like goods placed in the house. He used 'condition' here and 'specification' for crops to indicate there is no difference, and that this condition is not invalidating. He specified fruit following his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever buys land containing palm trees, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates [otherwise]', without distinguishing between pollinated and unpollinated. And it stands in place of specification; and the condition is if he says, 'With every little and much that is in it', unless he specifies by saying 'from its rights or from its appurtenances'; because rights are mentioned from it like the path and drinking place, and appurtenances are what one benefits from like the watercourse. It is a dependent inseparable from the sold item. And al-ta'bir: that one splits the vessel of a female date palm and puts some pollen from a male date palm into it, and it is improvement of fruit. According to the three [imams], if the fruit is not pollinated it enters, otherwise not. By qiyas, crops and fruit would enter, and the basis of qiyas is considering absolute connection to the sold item even if not for stability. (Ayni, Fath) 'By condition': because their connection is for stability, so they become like goods placed in the house. 'And it is said to the seller etc.': i.e., after the buyer has paid the price: 'Cut the crops and fruit, and deliver the land and the tree', because the buyer's property is occupied by the seller's property, so it is upon him to vacate and deliver it, just as if there were goods placed on it. And al-Shafi'i said: it is left until the fruit becomes ripe—i.e., animals or humans can benefit from it—and the crop is harvested, because the required delivery is the customary one, and customarily it is not cut like that. And it becomes as when a lease period ends and there is unripe crop on the land, it is left until harvest. And Malik and Ahmad said the same. Our evidence is that delivery is obligatory by the contract, unlike lease, because it is for usufruct, so it is left for an equivalent rent. =
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This lengthy section from Kanz al-Daqa'iq (with commentary) lays out the Hanafi rules on what automatically transfers in a sale of land or a house without explicit mention. The guiding principle is that anything permanently attached for stability (اتصال قرار) is included, while movable items are not unless a custom or condition includes them. The section begins with keys and locks: if the lock is fixed to the door (like a wooden bolt), the keys enter the sale; if the lock is separate (like a padlock), they do not. This is by istihsan, because keys are useless without locks. Next, buildings and structures like stairs, millstones, wells, and gardens are included by virtue of the hadith 'Whoever owns land owns what is above it...'. Trees on land are automatically included (even if small or fruitless), but dry trees are not. Crops and fruits are not included unless explicitly mentioned or conditioned, because they are connected for separation (for harvest), not for stability. However, if the crop is underground (like leeks) and remains for years, it is like a tree and enters. A 'tattimah' lists items included by custom: clothes of slaves (unless fancy), camel nose-ring, donkey rope, packsaddle (but not saddle), and offspring of animals if brought with the mother to the sale. Finally, the passage records a dispute with al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad: they say the buyer must leave the fruit and crops until ripe/harvest, because customary delivery does not require immediate cutting. The Hanafis respond that delivery (taslim) is an obligation of the contract, so the seller must vacate the property immediately; this differs from a lease where the land is for usufruct.

Key Terms
تبع (tabi'): Something that follows the main sold item in the contract, entering without separate mention
اتصال قرار (ittisal qarar): Permanent attachment for stability, as opposed to temporary attachment
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to include keys despite qiyas

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following items automatically enters the sale of a house without mention?
A) A key to a padlock resting on the front door.
B) A built-in staircase.
C) A portable air conditioner.
D) A potted plant on the windowsill.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for automatic inclusion in a sale is based on connection for stability (ittisal qarar); keys are an exception by istihsan, but only if the lock is fixed to the building.
